His attire is formal, with a necktie and lapel." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kierkegaard.jpg/220px-Kierkegaard.jpg" width="220" height="326" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Kierkegaard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Kierkegaard.jpg 2x" /></a><br />

style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Urs_von_Balthasar" title="Hans Urs von Balthasar">Urs von Balthasar</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Jean_Wahl" title="Jean Wahl">Wahl</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Welsh" title="Irvine Welsh">Welsh</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Cornel_West" title="Cornel West">West</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Wittgenstein</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">·</span> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wright_(author)" title="Richard Wright (author)">Wright</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr class="">
<th scope="row" style="text-align:left;">Signature</th>
<td class="" style=""><a href="/wiki/File:Kierkegaard_sig.png" class="image"><img alt="Signature, which reads: &quot;S. Kierkegaard.&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Kierkegaard_sig.png/128px-Kierkegaard_sig.png" width="128" height="37" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Kierkegaard_sig.png/192px-Kierkegaard_sig.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Kierkegaard_sig.png 2x" /></a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Søren Aabye Kierkegaard</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">s</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɔr/ 'or' in 'born'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɔr</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ən</span></a></span> <span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɪər/ 'ear' in 'beard'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɪər</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ə</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'g' in 'guy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɡ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɑr/ 'ar' in 'bard'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɑr</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'d' in 'dye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">d</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɪər/ 'ear' in 'beard'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɪər</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ə</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'g' in 'guy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɡ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɔr/ 'or' in 'born'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɔr</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>; <small>Danish:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Danish" title="Help:IPA for Danish">[ˈsɶːɐn ˈkiɐ̯ɡəɡɒːˀ]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<a href="/wiki/File:DA-S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard.ogg" title="File:DA-Søren Kierkegaard.ogg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" width="13" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" /></a> <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/DA-S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard.ogg" class="internal" title="DA-Søren Kierkegaard.ogg">listen</a>)</small>) (5 May 1813 – 11 November 1855) was a Danish <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poet" title="Poet">poet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_critic" title="Social critic" class="mw-redirect">social critic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> author. He wrote critical texts on <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, morality, ethics, psychology and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">philosophy of religion</a>, displaying a fondness for metaphor, irony and parables. He is widely considered to be the first <a href="/wiki/Existentialist" title="Existentialist" class="mw-redirect">existentialist</a> philosopher.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Much of his philosophical work deals with the issues of how one lives as a "single individual", giving priority to concrete human reality over abstract thinking, and highlighting the importance of personal choice and commitment.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a fierce critic of <a href="/wiki/Idealist" title="Idealist" class="mw-redirect">idealist</a> intellectuals and philosophers of his time, such as <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Schlegel" title="Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel">Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel</a>, as well as Danish pastors <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Peter_Mynster" title="Jacob Peter Mynster">Jacob Peter Mynster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Lassen_Martensen" title="Hans Lassen Martensen">Hans Lassen Martensen</a> and Danish poet <a href="/wiki/Johan_Ludvig_Heiberg_(poet)" title="Johan Ludvig Heiberg (poet)">Johan Ludvig Heiberg</a>.</p>
<p>His theological work focuses on <a href="/wiki/Christian_ethics" title="Christian ethics">Christian ethics</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">institution of the Church</a>, and on the differences between purely objective <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">proofs of Christianity</a> and the individual's subjective relationship to <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ" class="mw-redirect">Jesus Christ</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> the God-Man, which came through faith.<sup id="cite_ref-Elmer_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmer-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Much of his work deals with the art of Christian love. He was extremely critical of the practice of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> as a state religion, primarily that of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Denmark" title="Church of Denmark">Church of Denmark</a>. His psychological work explored the <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feelings</a> of individuals when faced with life choices.<sup id="cite_ref-Ostenfeld_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ostenfeld-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Kierkegaard's early work was written under various <a href="/wiki/Pseudonym" title="Pseudonym">pseudonyms</a> which he used to present distinctive viewpoints and interact with each other in complex dialogue.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> He assigned pseudonyms to explore particular viewpoints in-depth, which required several books in some instances, while Kierkegaard, openly or under another pseudonym, critiqued that position. He wrote many <i>Upbuilding Discourses</i> under his own name and dedicated them to the "single individual" who might want to discover the meaning of his works. Notably, he wrote: "<a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_method" title="Scholarly method">scholarship</a> want to teach that becoming objective is the way. Christianity teaches that the way is to become subjective, to become a subject."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The scientist can learn about the world by observation but Kierkegaard emphatically denied that observation could reveal the inner workings of the spiritual world.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1847 Kierkegaard described his own view of the single individual:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">God is not like a human being; it is not important for God to have visible evidence so that he can see if his cause has been victorious or not; he sees in secret just as well. Moreover, it is so far from being the case that you should help God to learn anew that it is rather he who will help you to learn anew, so that you are weaned from the worldly point of view that insists on visible evidence. (...) A decision in the external sphere is what Christianity does not want; (...) rather it wants to test the individual’s faith."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Some of Kierkegaard's key ideas include the concept of <i>"<a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard#Subjectivity" title="Philosophy of Søren Kierkegaard">Truth as Subjectivity</a>"</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_faith" title="Knight of faith">knight of faith</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Repetition_(Kierkegaard)" title="Repetition (Kierkegaard)">recollection and repetition</a> dichotomy, <a href="/wiki/Angst" title="Angst">angst</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Infinite_qualitative_distinction" title="Infinite qualitative distinction">infinite qualitative distinction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Theology of Søren Kierkegaard">faith as a passion</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard#Three_stages_of_life" title="Philosophy of Søren Kierkegaard">three stages on life's way</a>. Kierkegaard's writings were written in <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and were initially limited to <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, but by the turn of the 20th century, his writings were translated into major <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> languages, such as <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>. By the mid-20th century, his thought exerted a substantial influence on philosophy,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> theology,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> and Western culture.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_.281813.E2.80.931836.29">Early years (1813–1836)</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early years (1813–1836)">edit</a>]</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A sketch, featuring brown and white oils depicting a man sitting at a cafe table, writing on a piece of paper. A waitress is serving the man with a cup." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg/220px-Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg/330px-Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg/440px-Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kierkegaard_olavius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf3/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Kierkegaard in a coffee-house, an oil sketch by Christian Olavius, 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Søren Kierkegaard was born to an affluent family in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>. His mother, Ane Sørensdatter Lund Kierkegaard, had served as a maid in the household before marrying his father, Michael Pedersen Kierkegaard. She was an unassuming figure: quiet, plain, and not formally educated but Henriette Lund, her granddaughter, wrote that she "wielded the sceptre with joy and protected [Soren and Peter] like a hen protecting her children".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> His father was a "very stern man, to all appearances dry and prosaic, but under his 'rustic cloak' demeanor he concealed an active imagination which not even his great age could blunt."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> He read the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard preferred the comedies of <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Holberg" title="Ludvig Holberg">Ludvig Holberg</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> the writings of <a href="/wiki/Georg_Johann_Hamann" title="Georg Johann Hamann" class="mw-redirect">Georg Johann Hamann</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">Gotthold Ephraim Lessing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Young" title="Edward Young">Edward Young</a><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, especially those referring to Socrates.</p>
<p>Copenhagen in the 1830s and 1840s had crooked streets where carriages rarely went. Kierkegaard loved to walk them. In 1848, Kierkegaard wrote, "I had real Christian satisfaction in the thought that, if there were no other, there was definitely one man in Copenhagen whom every poor person could freely accost and converse with on the street; that, if there were no other, there was one man who, whatever the society he most commonly frequented, did not shun contact with the poor, but greeted every maidservant he was acquainted with, every manservant, every common laborer."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Our_Lady_(Copenhagen)" title="Church of Our Lady (Copenhagen)">Our Lady's Church</a> was at one end of the city, where <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Peter_Mynster" title="Jacob Peter Mynster">Bishop Mynster</a> preached the Gospel. At the other end was the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Theatre" title="Royal Danish Theatre">Royal Theatre</a> where <a href="/wiki/Johanne_Luise_Heiberg" title="Johanne Luise Heiberg">Fru Heiberg</a> performed.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Based on a speculative interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Anecdote" title="Anecdote">anecdotes</a> in Kierkegaard's unpublished journals, especially a rough draft of a story called "The Great Earthquake",<sup id="cite_ref-Watkin_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watkin-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> some early Kierkegaard scholars argued that Michael believed he had earned God's wrath and that none of his children would outlive him. He is said to have believed that his personal sins, perhaps indiscretions such as cursing the name of God in his youth or impregnating Ane out of wedlock, necessitated this punishment. Though five of his seven children died before he did, both Kierkegaard and his brother <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kierkegaard" title="Peter Kierkegaard">Peter Christian Kierkegaard</a> outlived him.<sup id="cite_ref-GarffBio_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GarffBio-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Peter, who was seven years Kierkegaard's elder, later became bishop in <a href="/wiki/Aalborg" title="Aalborg">Aalborg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GarffBio_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GarffBio-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Kierkegaard attended the School of Civic Virtue, Østre Borgerdyd Gymnasium, in 1830 when the school was situated in Klarebodeme, where he studied Latin and history among other subjects. He went on to study <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Copenhagen" title="University of Copenhagen">University of Copenhagen</a>. He had little interest in historical works, philosophy dissatisfied him, and he couldn't see "dedicating himself to <a href="/wiki/Philosophy#Main_theories" title="Philosophy">Speculation</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> He said, "What I really need to do is to get clear about "<a href="/wiki/Praxis_(process)" title="Praxis (process)">what am I to do</a>", not <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">what I must know</a>". He wanted to "lead a completely human life and not merely one of knowledge."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard didn't want to be a philosopher in the traditional or Hegelian sense<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> and he didn't want to preach a Christianity that was an illusion.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> "But he had learned from his father that one can do what one wills, and his father's life had not discredited this theory."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> He became a "spy for God". In 1848 Kierkegaard wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">Supposing that I had been free to use my talents as I pleased (and that it was not the case that another Power was able to compel me every moment when I was not ready to yield to fair means), I might from the first moment have converted my whole productivity into the channel of the interests of the age, it would have been in my power (if such betrayal were not punished by reducing me to naught) to become what the age demands, and so would have been (<a href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe" class="mw-redirect">Goetheo</a>-<a href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel" class="mw-redirect">Hegelian</a>) one more testimony to the proposition that the world is good, that the race is the truth and that this generation is the court of last resort, that the public is the discoverer of the truth and its judge, &amp;c. For by this treason I should have attained extraordinary success in the world. Instead of this I became (under compulsion) a spy.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>One of the first physical descriptions of Kierkegaard comes from an attendee, Hans Brøchner, at his brother Peter's wedding party in 1836: "I found [his appearance] almost comical. He was then twenty-three years old; he had something quite irregular in his entire form and had a strange coiffure. His hair rose almost six inches above his forehead into a tousled crest that gave him a strange, bewildered look."<sup id="cite_ref-Brochner_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brochner-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Kierkegaard's mother "was a nice little woman with an even and happy disposition," according to a grandchild's description. She was never mentioned in Kierkegaard's works. Ane died on 31 July 1834, age 66, possibly from <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> His father died on 8 August 1838, age 82. On 11 August, Kierkegaard wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">My father died on Wednesday (the 8th) at 2:00&#160;am I so deeply desired that he might have lived a few years more, and I regard his death as the last sacrifice of his love for me, because in dying he did not depart from me but he died for me, in order that something, if possible, might still come of me. Most precious of all that I have inherited from him is his memory, his transfigured image, transfigured not by his poetic imagination (for it does not need that), but transfigured by many little single episodes I am now learning about, and this memory I will try to keep most secret from the world. Right now I feel there is only one person (E. Boesen) with whom I can really talk about him. He was a "faithful friend."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Troels_Frederik_Lund" title="Troels Frederik Lund">Troels Frederik Lund</a>, his nephew, provided biographers with much information regarding Søren Kierkegaard.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Journals">Journals</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Journals">edit</a>]</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png" class="image"><img alt="Title page of a book, headed &quot;THE JOURNALS OF SØREN KIERKEGAARD&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png/220px-Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png" width="220" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png/330px-Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png/440px-Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cover_journals_kierkegaard.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf3/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The cover of the first English edition of <i>The Journals</i>, edited by Alexander Dru in 1938</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">People understand me so little that they do not even understand when I complain of being misunderstood.</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">—Søren Kierkegaard ,&#160; <cite><i>Journals</i> Feb. 1836</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard's Journals were first given to his brother-in-law, J.C. Lund and then to his brother, Peter Kierkegaard, but serious work on them began in 1865. H.P Barnum translated 1833–1846<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> but "threw away a significant portion of the originals."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> This rendered the journals up to 1847 more of a <a href="/wiki/Secondary_source" title="Secondary source">secondary source</a> of information about Kierkegaard than a <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary source</a>.</p>
<p>However, according to <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Bergmann" title="Hugo Bergmann">Samuel Hugo Bergmann</a>, "Kierkegaard's journals are one of the most important sources for an understanding of his philosophy".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard wrote over 7,000 pages in his journals on events, musings, thoughts about his works and everyday remarks.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> The entire collection of Danish journals was edited and published in 13 volumes consisting of 25 separate bindings including indices. The first English edition of the journals was edited by Alexander Dru in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-Dru_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dru-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The style is "literary and poetic [in] manner".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard saw his journals as his legacy:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">I have never confided in anyone. By being an author I have in a sense made the public my confidant. But in respect of my relation to the public I must, once again, make posterity my confidant. The same people who are there to laugh at one cannot very well be made one's confidant.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard's journals were the source of many <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorisms</a> credited to the philosopher. The following passage, from 1 August 1835, is perhaps his most oft-quoted aphorism and a key quote for existentialist studies:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">What I really need is to get clear about what I must do, not what I must know, except insofar as knowledge must precede every act. What matters is to find a purpose, to see what it really is that God wills that I shall do; the crucial thing is to find a truth which is truth for me, to find the idea for which I am willing to live and die."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Although his journals clarify some aspects of his work and life, Kierkegaard took care not to reveal too much. Abrupt changes in thought, repetitive writing, and unusual turns of phrase are some among the many tactics he used to throw readers off track. Consequently, there are many varying interpretations of his journals. Kierkegaard did not doubt the importance his journals would have in the future. In December 1849, he wrote: "Were I to die now the effect of my life would be exceptional; much of what I have simply jotted down carelessly in the Journals would become of great importance and have a great effect; for then people would have grown reconciled to me and would be able to grant me what was, and is, my right."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regine_Olsen_and_graduation_.281837.E2.80.931841.29">Regine Olsen and graduation (1837–1841)</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Regine Olsen and graduation (1837–1841)">edit</a>]</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regine_Olsen" title="Regine Olsen">Regine Olsen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Regine_olsen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of a young lady, over a black background. She is wearing a green dress, over a black coat. She is looking to the left, somewhat smiling." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Regine_olsen.jpg/170px-Regine_olsen.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Regine_olsen.jpg/255px-Regine_olsen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Regine_olsen.jpg/340px-Regine_olsen.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Regine_olsen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf3/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Regine Olsen, a muse for Kierkegaard's writings</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An important aspect of Kierkegaard's life - generally considered to have had a major influence on his work - was his broken engagement to <a href="/wiki/Regine_Olsen" title="Regine Olsen">Regine Olsen</a> (1822–1904). Kierkegaard and Olsen met on 8 May 1837 and were instantly attracted to each other, but sometime around 11 August 1838 he had second thoughts. In his journals, Kierkegaard wrote about his love for her:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">You, sovereign queen of my heart, Regina, hidden in the deepest secrecy of my breast, in the fullness of my life-idea, there where it is just as far to heaven as to hell—unknown divinity! O, can I really believe the poets when they say that the first time one sees the beloved object he thinks he has seen her long before, that love like all knowledge is recollection, that love in the single individual also has its prophecies, its types, its myths, its Old Testament. Everywhere, in the face of every girl, I see features of your beauty, but I think I would have to possess the beauty of all the girls in the world to extract your beauty, that I would have to sail around the world to find the portion of the world I want and toward which the deepest secret of my self polarically points—and in the next moment you are so close to me, so present, so overwhelmingly filling my spirit that I am transfigured to myself and feel that here it is good to be. You blind god of erotic love! You who see in secret, will you disclose it to me? Will I find what I am seeking here in this world, will I experience the conclusion of all my life's eccentric premises, will I fold you in my arms, or: <i>Do the Orders say: March on?</i> Have you gone on ahead, you, my longing, transfigured do you beckon to me from another world? O, I will throw everything away in order to become light enough to follow you. <i>Journals &amp; Papers of Søren Kierkegaard</i> 11 August 1838 <sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On 8 September 1840, Kierkegaard formally proposed to Olsen. He soon felt disillusioned about his prospects. He broke off the engagement on 11 August 1841, though it is generally believed that the two were deeply in love. In his journals, Kierkegaard mentions his belief that his "melancholy" made him unsuitable for marriage, but his precise motive for ending the engagement remains unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-GarffBio_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GarffBio-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HannayBio_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HannayBio-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The following quote from his <i>Stages on Life's Way</i> (1845) sheds some light on the motivation.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">
<blockquote>
<p>So it is a year ago today since I saw her for the first time, that is, for the first time with a resolute soul. I was no fantasizer, was not in the habit of becoming intoxicated on fine words and brief dreams; therefore my <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/resolution" class="extiw" title="wikt:resolution">resolution</a> certainly did not mean that I would die if she did not become mine. Neither did I think that my soul would be scattered and my life become completely empty for me if she did not become mine-I had too many religious <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/presupposition" class="extiw" title="wikt:presupposition">presuppositions</a> for that. For me my resolution meant: Marry her or do not marry at all. That is what was at stake. In my soul there was no doubt that I loved her, but I also knew that in connection with such a step there were so many anomalies that for me it became the most difficult task. An individuality like me is not nimble; I cannot say: If I do not have this one, I’ll take another. I do not dare to allow myself the presupposition, which comes easily to many, that a person is himself always all right, if only the other one is worthy of him. As far as I am concerned, the emphasis must be placed elsewhere-whether I was actually capable of giving my life the kind of expression that a marriage requires. I was as much in love as anyone, even though not many would understand that I, if my deliberation had not allowed me this step, would have kept my falling in love to myself. I marry her or I do not marry at all. Soren Kierkegaard, <i>Stages on Life's Way</i> p. 195–196</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>About this conflict within me, I do not dare as yet to say "today,"<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> but I feel that I owe her much with respect to venturing. The one who has made another person unhappy can be very useful for persevering in battles such as this: the one who is sentenced to life is given rasp work to do, which is mortally dangerous, but then, after all, he is condemned.This, too, I also realize-that the unmarried person can venture more in the world of the spirit than the married, and risk everything and be concerned only about the idea, and is qualified in a totally different way to be in the <i>discrimen</i> [crucial movement] of decision, where it is almost impossible to stand, to say nothing of taking up residence. But definitely this was not my reason for not wishing to marry. Indeed, I, too, wished for a somewhat quieter happiness in life, and her beseeching made my own wish my one and only wish. And even if I had not wished it, I would have done it, because I always believe that obedience is more precious to God than, cosmopolitan, philanthropic, patriotic sacrifices upon the altar of humanity, that quietness in the fulfilling of a modest duty is infinitely more valuable and more befitting to every human being than luxuriance in the world of the spirit and prodigality of concern for the whole human race, as if one were God in heaven. Let them talk vehemently about God’s wrath and the consuming fire ({Hebrews 12.29})-there is also something I fear and fear just as much, and that is that I might force God to pull rank on me, make me vanish as a lie before his stately and superior majesty. As soon as I disdain duty, God becomes exclusive, for only in duty am I in humble harmony with his sublimity, and therefore his majesty is not exclusiveness. Therefore it is not God who makes himself exclusive which he never does (this is paganism), but it is I who make him exclusive, and this is a punishment. This is the profound consistency: that the person who wants to come closer to God by disdaining what is simple, distances God in his exclusiveness, in an exclusiveness that not even the most wretched of human beings has to experience. Here, too, I am quick of hearing, and even though <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">many a philosopher who shouts to the world give me a place to stand</a> does not hear it, I hear a voice that says: I will give you your <i>Dosmoi</i>, you dunces [<i>Dosmere</i>]! No! if I had not believed that I had a divine counterorder, I never would have retreated, and as soon as this order is revoked I shall choose my wish again. God forbid that the effort and tension weaken my wish before this is allowed! My counterorder I can understand, for it goes through repentance. A repentant individuality who is able to take a whole lifetime to recant cannot advance. This is a very simple protest against marriage. I have neither visions nor dreams to guide me; my collision is quite simply the collision of repentance with existence, a collision of suspension with a present actuality. Until it is resolved, I am in <i>suspenso</i>; as soon as it is resolved, I am free again. This is why I am doing all I can to keep myself at the pinnacle of love. As soon as she is free, the religious crisis are my task. Just suppose, to think quite theoretically, suppose that she found herself again; just suppose that the remark about death had been only an exaggeration, not intended as a pathos-filled reply but something like the utterance when one says in the course of a conversation: I am almost dying from the heat in this cramped apartment. Just suppose she had meant it but did not understand herself, or suppose she had suffered unto death but had been victorious; just suppose I had contributed a little to this victory or nothing at all; just suppose she seized the defense that she had never cared about me at all-what then? Good God, as if this were possible! How my soul snatches at every explanation from this quarter! ... You see, if this was the situation, then my position becomes difficult in an odd way. I would have to have a human life on my conscience in order to be awakened and torn out of the lethargy of depression. I humble myself under the earnestness of this thought. But then along comes my understanding and says: No, this is not true; you perceived, of course that it was not a question of a human life, it was your imagination that created this hallucination and showed it to your depression, and both agreed that it was possible. But then it was not a human life; it was a word that you perhaps would even have laughed at in many another’s mouth. Yes, in one sense that is true. And yet I regret nothing; I do not regret having suffered all this pain, which nevertheless has not yet paralyzed me, although it would do that if I were to talk about it. I have felt it in loneliness, in sleeplessness, when in one second one can think more thoughts together than one can write in months, when the <a href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Anxiety" title="The Concept of Anxiety">imagination conjures up anxieties</a> with which no pen dares to become involved, when the conscience gives a start of alarm and terrifies with optical illusions. But alas, all this is only a theory. To grasp a theory is just like embracing a cloud instead of Juno, and it is also unfaithfulness to her. But to use the theory as a means of exercising, to unbuckle the soul in it so as to give one’s energy new elasticity, that is permissible-indeed it is what one ought to do. Soren Kierkegaard, <i>Stages on Life's Way</i> p. 259-261, 262, 265ff Hong<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard turned attention to his examinations. On 13 May 1839 he wrote, "I have no alternative than to suppose that it is God's will that I prepare for my examination and that it is more pleasing to him that I do this than actually coming to some clearer perception by immersing myself in one or another sort of research, for obedience is more precious to him than the fat of rams."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The death of his father and the death of <a href="/wiki/Poul_Martin_M%C3%B8ller" title="Poul Martin Møller">Poul Møller</a> also played a part in his decision. Kierkegaard may have been thinking about this quote by Møller as he contemplated the completion of his examinations.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"What Poul Møller says somewhere is so true and so mature: "During fairly complete idleness, one can still avoid boredom as long as an obligatory task is being neglected through the idleness, because one is then somewhat occupied by the continual struggle one is in with oneself. But as soon as the duty ceases, or one no longer feels any reminder of it at all, boredom sets in. The private tutor who from moment to moment postpones a working hour enjoys himself as long as he is on the point of going to his pupil, but when he has decided to skip the hour his enjoyment ceases. The reminder by conscience in that example was something unpleasant that served as stimulation for something pleasant. A poet who is writing a tragedy, although it was part of his plan of life to study for a degree, will do it with greater enthusiasm than he will do it later if he gives up that plan."" Soren Kierkegaard, <i>The Book on Adler</i> p. 128–129 Hong 1998</p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 29 September 1841, Kierkegaard wrote and defended his dissertation, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Concept_of_Irony_with_Continual_Reference_to_Socrates" title="On the Concept of Irony with Continual Reference to Socrates">On the Concept of Irony with Continual Reference to Socrates</a></i>. The university panel considered it noteworthy and thoughtful, but too informal and witty for a serious academic thesis.<sup id="cite_ref-irony_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irony-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> The thesis dealt with <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Schelling's</a> 1841 lectures, which Kierkegaard had attended with <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>; each had come away with a different perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard graduated from university on 20 October 1841 with a Magister Artium, which today would be designated a PhD. He was able to fund his education, his living, and several publications of his early works with his family's inheritance of approximately 31,000 <a href="/wiki/Danish_rigsdaler" title="Danish rigsdaler">rigsdaler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dru_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dru-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authorship_.281843.E2.80.931844.29">Authorship (1843–1844)</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Authorship (1843–1844)">edit</a>]</span></h2>
<p>Kierkegaard published some of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.kierkegaard.com/chrono.html">his works</a> using <a href="/wiki/Pseudonyms" title="Pseudonyms" class="mw-redirect">pseudonyms</a> and for others he signed his own name as author. Pseudonyms were used often in the early 19th century as a means of representing viewpoints other than the author's own; examples include the writers of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Papers" title="Federalist Papers">Federalist Papers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalist_Papers" title="Anti-Federalist Papers">Anti-Federalist Papers</a>. Kierkegaard employed the same technique as a way to provide examples of indirect communication.</p>
<p><i>On the Concept of Irony with Continual Reference to Socrates</i> was his university thesis, mentioned above. His first book, <i>De omnibus dubitandum est</i> (<i>Latin:</i> "Everything must be doubted"), was written in 1841–42 but was not published until after his death. It was written under the pseudonym "Johannes Climacus".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Either/Or" title="Either/Or">Either/Or</a></i> was published 20 February 1843; it was mostly written during Kierkegaard's stay in Berlin, where he took notes on Schelling's <i>Philosophy of Revelation</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Edited by Victor Eremita, the book contained the papers of an unknown "A" and "B" which were discovered by Victor Eremita (the pseudonymous author) claimed to have found these papers in a secret drawer of his <a href="/wiki/Secretary_desk" title="Secretary desk">secretary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Eremita had a hard time putting the papers of "A" in order because they were not straightforward. "B"'s papers were arranged in an orderly fashion. <sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Both these characters are trying to become religious individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Each approach the idea of first <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a> from an <a href="/wiki/Esthetic" title="Esthetic" class="mw-redirect">esthetic</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Ethic" title="Ethic" class="mw-redirect">ethical</a> point of view. The book is basically an argument about <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> with a short discourse at the end telling them they should stop arguing. Eremita thinks "B", a judge, makes the most sense.</p>
<p>Three months after the publication of <i>Either/Or</i>, 16 May 1843, he published <i>Two Edifying Discourses</i> and continued to publish discourses along with his <a href="/wiki/Pseudonymous" title="Pseudonymous" class="mw-redirect">pseudonymous</a> books. These discourses were published under Kierkegaard's own name and are available as <i>Eighteen Upbuilding Discourses</i> today.</p>
<p>Edifying is not a word that is used much today but it was when David F. Swenson translated the works in the 1940s. Later, in 1990, <a href="/wiki/Howard_V._and_Edna_H._Hong_Kierkegaard_Library" title="Howard V. and Edna H. Hong Kierkegaard Library">Howard V. and Edna H. Hong</a> translated the works again but used the word upbuilding, which was more in line with Kierkegaard's thought after 1846 when he wrote Christian <a href="/wiki/Deliberations" title="Deliberations" class="mw-redirect">deliberations</a> about <a href="/wiki/Works_of_Love" title="Works of Love">works of love</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> An upbuilding discourse or edifying discourse isn't the same as a sermon because a sermon is preached to a congregation while a discourse can be carried on between several people or even with oneself. The discourse or conversation should be "upbuilding", which means one would build up the other person, or oneself, rather than tear down in order to build up. Kierkegaard said: "Although this little book (which is called "<a href="/wiki/Conversation" title="Conversation">discourses</a>," not <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, because its author does not have <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> to <i>preach</i>, "upbuilding discourses," not discourses for upbuilding, because the speaker by no means claims to be a <i>teacher</i>) wishes to be only what it is, a <a class="external text" href="http://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/superfluity">superfluity</a>, and desires only to remain in hiding".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 16 October 1843 Kierkegaard published three more books about love and faith and several more discourses. <i><a href="/wiki/Fear_and_Trembling" title="Fear and Trembling">Fear and Trembling</a></i>, published under the pseudonym Johannes de Silentio, was about Abraham wishing he did not have to sacrifice his son, <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a>, to show his love for God. Kierkegaard compared Abraham's situation with that of <a href="/wiki/Agamemnon" title="Agamemnon">Agamemnon</a> who saw it as his duty to sacrifice his daughter, <a href="/wiki/Iphigenia" title="Iphigenia">Iphigenia</a>, to show his love for the Greek state. Abraham couldn't understand how sacrificing Isaac would be a good gift while Agamemnon was certain that sacrificing Iphigenia would be a good gift to the Greek state. <i><a href="/wiki/Repetition_(Kierkegaard)" title="Repetition (Kierkegaard)">Repetition</a></i> is about a Young Man (Soren Kierkegaard) who is suffering from <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a> and depression because he feels he has to sacrifice his love for a girl (Regine Olsen) to God. He tries to see if the new science of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> can help him understand himself. Constantin Constantius, who is the pseudonymous author of that book, is the psychologist. At the same time he published <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Upbuilding_Discourses" title="Three Upbuilding Discourses">Three Upbuilding Discourses</a>, 1843</i> under his own name, which dealt specifically with how love can be used to hide things from yourself or others.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> These three books, all published on the same day, are an example of Kierkegaard's method of indirect communication.</p>
<p>Kierkegaard questioned whether an individual can know if something is a good gift from God or not and concludes by saying, "it does not depend, then, merely upon what one sees, but what one sees depends upon <i>how</i> one sees; all observation is not just a receiving, a discovering, but also a bringing forth, and insofar as it is that, how the observer himself is <a href="/wiki/Temperament" title="Temperament">constituted</a> is indeed decisive."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> God's love is imparted indirectly just as our own sometimes is. He wrote the following in 1845, 1846, and in 1847.</p>
<blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px; display:table;">
<p>"God creates out of nothing, but here, if I dare say so, he does more-he dresses an instinct in all the beauty of erotic love so that the lovers see only the beauty and are unaware of the instinct." .... "The how of the truth is precisely the truth. .... Every human being is spirit and truth is the self-activity of appropriation." .... "There is no work, not one single one, not even the best, about which we unconditionally dare to say: The one who does this unconditionally demonstrates love by it. It depends on <i>how</i> the work is done." Soren Kierkegaard, <i>Stages on Life's Way</i>, p. 122-123, <i>Concluding Postscript</i> p. 322-323, 242, <i>Works of Love</i>, Hong p. 13</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1844 he published two, three, four upbuilding discourses just as he did in 1843 but here he discussed how an individual might come to know <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. <a href="/wiki/Theologians" title="Theologians" class="mw-redirect">Theologians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophers" title="Philosophers" class="mw-redirect">philosophers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historians" title="Historians" class="mw-redirect">historians</a> were all engaged in <a href="/wiki/Debating" title="Debating" class="mw-redirect">debating</a> about the existence of God. This is direct communication and Kierkegaard thinks this might be useful for theologians, philosophers, and historians (associations) but not at all useful for the "single individual" that is interested in becoming a Christian. Kierkegaard always wrote for "that single individual whom I with joy and gratitude call <i>my</i> reader"<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> The single individual must put what is <a href="/wiki/Understood" title="Understood" class="mw-redirect">understood</a> to use or it will be lost. <a href="/wiki/Human_self-reflection" title="Human self-reflection">Reflection</a> can take an individual only so far before the <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a> begins to change the whole content of what was being thought about. Love is won by being exercised just as much as faith and patience are.</p>
<p>He also wrote several more pseudonymous books in 1844: <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Fragments" title="Philosophical Fragments">Philosophical Fragments</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Prefaces" title="Prefaces">Prefaces</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Anxiety" title="The Concept of Anxiety">The Concept of Anxiety</a></i> and finished the year up with <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Upbuilding_Discourses,_1844&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Four Upbuilding Discourses, 1844 (page does not exist)">Four Upbuilding Discourses, 1844</a></i>. He used indirect communication in the first book and direct communication in the rest of them. He doesn't believe the question about God's existence should be an <a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a> held by one group and differently by another no matter how many <a href="/wiki/Scientific_demonstration" title="Scientific demonstration">demonstrations</a> are made. He says its up to the single individual to make the <a href="/wiki/Fruit_of_the_Holy_Spirit" title="Fruit of the Holy Spirit">fruit of the Holy Spirit</a> real because love and joy are always just possibilities. <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> wanted to define God's <a href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Attributes of God in Christianity">attributes</a> once and for all but Kierkegaard was against this. His love for Regine was a disaster but it helped him because of his point of view. In 1845 he wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>There is one person, one single person, about whom everything revolves. I gaze and gaze so long at this girl-until I draw out of myself what I perhaps would otherwise never have come to see, even if I had seen ever so much, for this would not imply that my inwardness had become transparent to me. If she had been unusually gifted with spirit, she would never have affected me in this way. She is quite enough for me when it comes to responsibility, and again the responsibility is mine, and yet it is she who in this responsibility brings my inwardness to consciousness. The testing quietness of responsibility teaches a person to have to help himself by virtue of spirit; achievement, action, activities, so often lauded and deservedly so, can still have an admixture of <a class="external text" href="http://en.wikiquote.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Search&amp;search=diversion&amp;redirs=1&amp;profile=default">diversion</a> so that one does not find out what one is capable of by virtue of spirit and what the manifold external stimuli help one to attain; one also escapes many a terror that does not have time to reach one, but to escape them does not mean to have conquered them or to have understood oneself. She will go on helping me with responsibility, for I will not be finished where she is finished. Suppose she became another’s and I became free. Then I am not finished, for then I would still have the possibility that it would suddenly strike me-perhaps prompted by some thinker or by a chance word, which at times has the greatest power—that it would suddenly strike me that a marriage could have been built out of our relationship. Precisely because in that case I would not have the <a href="/wiki/Sympathy" title="Sympathy">sympathetic</a> consternation on her behalf, the pain would grip me again, but <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">auto</a><a href="/wiki/Pathos" title="Pathos">pathetically</a>. What will responsibility be to me then? It will become my very consolation, and in that very responsibility I shall come to <a href="/wiki/Understand" title="Understand" class="mw-redirect">understand</a> <a href="/wiki/Myself" title="Myself" class="mw-redirect">myself</a>. Soren Kierkegaard, <i>Stages on Life's Way</i>, Hong p. 363-368</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard believed "each <a href="/wiki/Generation" title="Generation">generation</a> has its own task and need not trouble itself unduly by being <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything#Theory_of_everything_and_philosophy" title="Theory of everything">everything</a> to previous and succeeding generations".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> In an earlier book he had said, "to a certain degree every generation and every individual begins his life from the beginning",<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> and in another, "no generation has learned to love from another, no generation is able to begin at any other point than the beginning", "no generation learns the essentially human from a previous one.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> He was against the <a href="/wiki/Hegelian" title="Hegelian" class="mw-redirect">Hegelian</a> idea of <a href="/wiki/Mediation_(Marxist_theory_and_media_studies)" title="Mediation (Marxist theory and media studies)">mediation</a><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> because it introduces a "third term"<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> that comes between the single individual and the object of desire. Kierkegaard asked if logic ends in actuality, can a person logically prove God's existence? <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a> says no. Then he turns from logic to <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> and finds that Hegelian philosophy is <a href="/wiki/Apophatic_theology" title="Apophatic theology">negative</a> rather than positive.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> This "third term" isn't mediation, it's love that proves God's existence. He addressed this again in 1847:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>You know that God is, but it seems as if he has withdrawn into himself. You know that Christ existed eighteen-hundred years ago, but that was a long time ago. Meet all the terrors of the future with this comfort: love abides; meet all the anxiety and listlessness of the present with this comfort; love abides. This is an upbuilding thought: love abides. This little work is continually dealing only with the works of love, therefore not with God’s love, but with human love. There is the one who loves then the object of love and love itself as the third. .... God’s being merciful points away from himself. "If you want to love me, then love the people you see; what you do for them, you do for me. If you want to show that your life is intended to serve God, then let it serve people, yet continually with the thought of God." Soren Kierkegaard, <i>Works of Love</i>, Hong, p. 301, 160-161</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hidden_Inwardness">Hidden Inwardness</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hidden Inwardness">edit</a>]</span></h3>
<p>Kierkegaard believed God comes to each individual mysteriously, he wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px; display:table;">
<p>"Revelation is marked by mystery, eternal happiness by suffering, the certitude of faith by uncertainty, easiness by difficulty, truth by absurdity; if this is not maintained, then the esthetic and the religious merge in common confusion. ... The religious lies in the dialectic of inwardness deepening and therefore, with regard to the conception of God, this means that he himself is moved, is changed. An action in the eternal transforms the individual’s existence." This was part of Kierkegaard's theory of "indirect communication." He wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"No anonymous author can more slyly hide himself, and no <a href="/wiki/Maieutic" title="Maieutic" class="mw-redirect">maieutic</a> can more carefully recede from a direct relation than God can. He is in the creation, everywhere in the creation, but he is not there directly, and only when the single individual turns inward into himself (consequently only in the inwardness of <a href="/wiki/Self-determination_theory" title="Self-determination theory">self-activity</a>) does he become <a href="/wiki/Aware" title="Aware" class="mw-redirect">aware</a> and capable of seeing God." <i>Concluding Unscientific Postscript</i>, Hong 1992 p. 243</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>Despite everything people ought to have learned about my maieutic carefulness, in addition to proceeding slowly and continually letting it seem as if I knew nothing more, not the next thing — now on the occasion of my new upbuilding discourses they will probably bawl out that I do not know what comes next, that I know nothing about sociality. You fools! Yet on the other hand I owe it to myself to confess before God that in a certain sense there is some truth in it, only not as men understand it — namely, that when I have first presented one aspect sharply, then I affirm the other even more strongly. Now I have my theme of the next book. It will be called: <i>Works of Love</i>. <i>Journals of Soren Kierkegaard</i> VIII1A4</p>
</blockquote>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard published <i>Three Discourses on Imagined Occasions</i> under his own name on 29 April, and <i><a href="/wiki/Stages_on_Life%27s_Way" title="Stages on Life's Way">Stages on Life's Way</a></i> edited by Hilarius Bookbinder, 30 April 1845. The <i>Stages</i> is a rewrite of Either/Or which Kierkegaard didn't think had been adequately read by the public and in <i>Stages</i> he predicted "that two-thirds of the book's readers will quit before they are halfway through, out of boredom they will throw the book away."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> He then went to Berlin for a short rest. Upon returning he published his <i>Discourses</i> of 1843–44 in one volume, <i>Eighteen Upbuilding Discourses</i>, 29 May 1845 and finished the first part of his authorship with <i><a href="/wiki/Concluding_Unscientific_Postscript_to_the_Philosophical_Fragments" title="Concluding Unscientific Postscript to the Philosophical Fragments" class="mw-redirect">Concluding Unscientific Postscript to the Philosophical Fragments</a></i> which was a rewrite of <i>Philosophical Fragments</i> as well as an explanation of the first part of his authorship. In 1851 he further explained himself in his <i>Journal</i>. "What I have understood as the task of the authorship has been done. It is one idea, this continuity from <i>Either/Or</i> to <a href="/wiki/Practice_in_Christianity" title="Practice in Christianity">Anti-Climacus</a>, the idea of religiousness in reflection. The task has occupied me totally, for it has occupied me religiously; I have understood the completion of this authorship as my duty, as a responsibility resting upon me." He advised his reader to read his books slowly and also to read them aloud since that might aid in understanding.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He was writing about the <a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">inner being</a> in all of these books and his goal was to get the single individual away from all the <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a> that was going on about God and <a href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>. Speculation creates quantities of ways to find God and his Goods but finding faith in Christ and putting the understanding to use stops all speculation because then one begins to actually exist as a Christian or in an ethical/religious way. In Kierkegaard's view the Church shouldn't try to prove Christianity or even defend it. It should help the single individual to make a <a href="/wiki/Leap_of_faith" title="Leap of faith">leap of faith</a>. He wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px; display:table;">
<p>It is in the living room that the battle must be fought, lest the skirmishes of religiousness become a changing-of-the-guard parade one day a week. It is in the living room that the battle must be fought, not imaginatively in church, with the pastor shadowboxing and the listeners looking on. It is in the living room that the battle must be fought, because the victory must be that the home becomes a shrine. Let the work be done directly in the church by holding an inspection of the contending forces-under whose banner the battle will be fought, in whose name the victory will be won-by describing the position of the enemy, by imitating the attack, by praising the omnipotent ally and strengthening trust by arousing mistrust, trust in him through mistrust of oneself. Let the work be done indirectly by the ironic but yet most tender sympathy of secret concern. But the main point still is that the single individual will go home from church willing wholeheartedly and eagerly to battle in the living room. If the pastor’s activity in the church is merely a once-a-week attempt to tow the congregation’s cargo ship a little closer to eternity, the whole thing comes to nothing, because a human life, unlike a cargo ship, cannot lie in the same place until the next Sunday. Therefore, the church is the very place where the difficulty must be presented, and it is better to go from the church discouraged and to find the task easier than one thought than to go from church overly confident and to become discouraged in the living room. <i>Concluding Unscientific Postscript to Philosophical Fragments</i> P. 465</p>
</blockquote>
<p>He described his early writing this way in 1846:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">"The movement: from the poet (from aesthetics), from philosophy (from speculation), to the indication of the most central definition of what Christianity is—from the pseudonymous ‘Either/Or’, through ‘The Concluding Postscript’ with my name as editor, to the ‘Discourses at Communion on Fridays’, two of which were delivered in the Church of our Lady. This movement was accomplished or described <i>uno tenore</i>, in one breath, if I may use this expression, so that the authorship integrally regarded, is religious from first to last—a thing which everyone can see if he is willing to see, and therefore ought to see."<sup id="cite_ref-PtView_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PtView-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Kierkegaard wrote his <i>Concluding Unscientific Postscript to Philosophical Fragments</i> in 1846 and here he tried to explain the intent of the first part of his authorship. He said, "Christianity will not be content to be an evolution within the total category of human nature; an engagement such as that is too little to offer to a god. Neither does it even want to be the paradox for the believer, and then surreptitiously, little by little, provide him with understanding, because the martyrdom of faith (to crucify one’s understanding) is not a martyrdom of the moment, but the martyrdom of continuance."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">"The highest His Imperial Highness is able to do is to make the decision before God. The lowliest human being can also make his decision before God. .... Irony is the cultivation of the spirit and therefore follows next after immediacy; then comes the ethicist, then the humorist, then the religious person. The religious person discovers that what engages him absolutely seems to engage others very little, but he draws no conclusions, partly because he has no time for that and partly because he cannot know for sure whether all these people are not <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_Faith" title="Knight of Faith" class="mw-redirect">knights of faith</a> in hidden inwardness. .... The human is hidden inwardness in absolute passion; here again is the implication that everyone else must be equally able to approach God, because the person who in absolute inwardness wants to be conscious of being a chosen one <i>eo ipso</i> lacks inwardness, since his life is comparative. ... In the religiousness of hidden inwardness, a person does not consider himself better than anyone else, does not consider himself distinguished by the relationship with God in a way that is not possible for everyone. .... The storing up of guilt cannot find its expression in any externality, whereby it is made finite; it belongs, therefore, to hidden inwardness. .... Scientific scholarship wants to take charge of the dialectical and to that end bring it over into the medium of abstraction, whereby the issue is again mistreated, since it is an existence-issue, and the actual dialectical difficulty disappears by being explained in the medium of abstraction, which ignores existence. If the turbulent religious address is for sentimental people who are quick to sweat and to be sweated out, then the speculative interpretation is for pure thinking; but neither of the two is for acting and, by virtue of acting, for existing human beings." <i>Concluding Unscientific Postscript to Philosophical Fragments</i>, Hong, p. 496-497, 501-505, 510, 538-539, 556</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Early Kierkegaardian scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Theodor_W._Adorno" title="Theodor W. Adorno">Theodor W. Adorno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Croxall" title="Thomas Henry Croxall">Thomas Henry Croxall</a> argue that the entire authorship should be treated as Kierkegaard's own personal and religious views.<sup id="cite_ref-Adorno_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adorno-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> This view leads to confusions and contradictions which make Kierkegaard appear philosophically incoherent.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcia_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcia-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Many later scholars, such as the <a href="/wiki/Post-structuralism" title="Post-structuralism">post-structuralists</a>, interpreted Kierkegaard's work by attributing the pseudonymous texts to their respective authors. <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_Christianity" title="Postmodern Christianity">Postmodern Christians</a> present a different interpretation of Kierkegaard's works.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Kierkegaard used the category of "The Individual"<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> to stop<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> the endless <i>Either/Or</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Kierkegaard's most important pseudonyms,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> in chronological order, were:</p>
<ul>
<li>Victor Eremita, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Either/Or" title="Either/Or">Either/Or</a></i></li>
<li>A, writer of many articles in <i>Either/Or</i></li>
<li>Judge William, author of rebuttals to A in <i>Either/Or</i></li>
<li>Johannes de silentio, author of <i><a href="/wiki/Fear_and_Trembling" title="Fear and Trembling">Fear and Trembling</a></i></li>
<li>Constantin Constantius, author of the first half of <i><a href="/wiki/Repetition_(Kierkegaard)" title="Repetition (Kierkegaard)">Repetition</a></i></li>
<li>Young Man, author of the second half of <i>Repetition</i></li>
<li>Vigilius Haufniensis, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Anxiety" title="The Concept of Anxiety">The Concept of Anxiety</a></i></li>
<li>Nicolaus Notabene, author of <i><a href="/wiki/Prefaces" title="Prefaces">Prefaces</a></i></li>
<li>Hilarius Bookbinder, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Stages_on_Life%27s_Way" title="Stages on Life's Way">Stages on Life's Way</a></i></li>
<li>Johannes Climacus, author of <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Fragments" title="Philosophical Fragments">Philosophical Fragments</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Concluding_Unscientific_Postscript" title="Concluding Unscientific Postscript" class="mw-redirect">Concluding Unscientific Postscript</a></i></li>
<li>Inter et Inter, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Crisis_and_a_Crisis_in_the_Life_of_an_Actress" title="The Crisis and a Crisis in the Life of an Actress">The Crisis and a Crisis in the Life of an Actress</a></i></li>
<li>H.H., author of <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Two_Ethical-Religious_Essays&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Two Ethical-Religious Essays (page does not exist)">Two Ethical-Religious Essays</a></i></li>
<li>Anti-Climacus, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sickness_Unto_Death" title="The Sickness Unto Death">The Sickness Unto Death</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Practice_in_Christianity" title="Practice in Christianity">Practice in Christianity</a></i></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Corsair_Affair">The Corsair Affair</span> <span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Corsair Affair">edit</a>]</span></h3>
<p>On 22 December 1845, <a href="/wiki/Peder_Ludvig_M%C3%B8ller" title="Peder Ludvig Møller">Peder Ludvig Møller</a>, a young "<a href="/wiki/Seducer" title="Seducer" class="mw-redirect">seducer</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> of Kierkegaard's generation who studied at the University of Copenhagen at the same time as Kierkegaard, published an article indirectly criticizing <i><a href="/wiki/Stages_on_Life%27s_Way" title="Stages on Life's Way">Stages on Life's Way</a></i>. The article complimented Kierkegaard for his wit and intellect, but questioned whether he would ever be able to master his talent and write coherent, complete works. Møller was also a contributor to and editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Corsaren" title="Corsaren">The Corsair</a></i>, a Danish satirical paper that lampooned everyone of notable standing. Kierkegaard published a sarcastic response, charging that Møller's article was merely an attempt to impress Copenhagen's literary elite.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A caricature; the figure is standing facing left, with a top-hat, cane, formal attire. The caricature is over-emphasizing his back, by making him appear as a hunchback." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg/170px-S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg" width="170" height="367" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard_i_Corsaren.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf3/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A caricature of Kierkegaard published in <i><a href="/wiki/Corsaren" title="Corsaren">The Corsair</a></i>, a satirical journal</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Kierkegaard wrote two small pieces in response to Møller, <i>The Activity of a Traveling Esthetician</i> and <i>Dialectical Result of a Literary Police Action</i>.
